        <title>The draft genome of Primula veris yields insights into the molecular basis of heterostyly | Genome Biology | Full Text</title>

        

    <meta name="citation_abstract" content="The flowering plant Primula veris is a common spring blooming perennial that is widely cultivated throughout Europe. This species is an established model system in the study of the genetics, evolution, and ecology of heterostylous floral polymorphisms. Despite the long history of research focused on this and related species, the continued development of this system has been restricted due the absence of genomic and transcriptomic resources. We present here a de novo draft genome assembly of P. veris covering 301.8&#160;Mb, or approximately 63% of the estimated 479.22&#160;Mb genome, with an N50 contig size of 9.5 Kb, an N50 scaffold size of 164 Kb, and containing an estimated 19,507 genes. The results of a RADseq bulk segregant analysis allow for the confident identification of four genome scaffolds that are linked to the P. veris S-locus. RNAseq data from both P. veris and the closely related species P. vulgaris allow for the characterization of 113 candidate heterostyly genes that show significant floral morph-specific differential expression. One candidate gene of particular interest is a duplicated GLOBOSA homolog that may be unique to Primula (PveGLO2), and is completely silenced in L-morph flowers. The P. veris genome represents the first genome assembled from a heterostylous species, and thus provides an immensely important resource for future studies focused on the evolution and genetic dissection of heterostyly. As the first genome assembled from the Primulaceae, the P. veris genome will also facilitate the expanded application of phylogenomic methods in this diverse family and the eudicots as a whole."/>

    <meta name="description" content="The flowering plant Primula veris is a common spring blooming perennial that is widely cultivated throughout Europe. This species is an established model system in the study of the genetics, evolution, and ecology of heterostylous floral polymorphisms. Despite the long history of research focused on this and related species, the continued development of this system has been restricted due the absence of genomic and transcriptomic resources. We present here a de novo draft genome assembly of P. veris covering 301.8&#160;Mb, or approximately 63% of the estimated 479.22&#160;Mb genome, with an N50 contig size of 9.5 Kb, an N50 scaffold size of 164 Kb, and containing an estimated 19,507 genes. As the first genome assembled from the Primulaceae, the P. veris genome will also facilitate the expanded application of phylogenomic methods in this diverse family and the eudicots as a whole."/>

        <meta property="og:url" content="https://genomebiology.biomedcentral.com/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z"/>
        <meta property="og:type" content="article"/>
        <meta property="og:site_name" content="Genome Biology"/>
        <meta property="og:title" content="The draft genome of Primula veris yields insights into the molecular basis of heterostyly"/>
        <meta property="og:description" content="The flowering plant Primula veris is a common spring blooming perennial that is widely cultivated throughout Europe. This species is an established model system in the study of the genetics, evolution, and ecology of heterostylous floral polymorphisms. Despite the long history of research focused on this and related species, the continued development of this system has been restricted due the absence of genomic and transcriptomic resources. We present here a de novo draft genome assembly of P. veris covering 301.8&amp;nbsp;Mb, or approximately 63% of the estimated 479.22&amp;nbsp;Mb genome, with an N50 contig size of 9.5 Kb, an N50 scaffold size of 164 Kb, and containing an estimated 19,507 genes. The results of a RADseq bulk segregant analysis allow for the confident identification of four genome scaffolds that are linked to the P. veris S-locus. RNAseq data from both P. veris and the closely related species P. vulgaris allow for the characterization of 113 candidate heterostyly genes that show significant floral morph-specific differential expression. One candidate gene of particular interest is a duplicated GLOBOSA homolog that may be unique to Primula (PveGLO2), and is completely silenced in L-morph flowers. The P. veris genome represents the first genome assembled from a heterostylous species, and thus provides an immensely important resource for future studies focused on the evolution and genetic dissection of heterostyly. As the first genome assembled from the Primulaceae, the P. veris genome will also facilitate the expanded application of phylogenomic methods in this diverse family and the eudicots as a whole."/>
        <meta property="og:image" content="https://media.springernature.com/w200/springer-static/cover/journal/13059.jpg"/>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Background</h3><p>The flowering plant <i>Primula veris</i> is a common spring blooming perennial that is widely cultivated throughout Europe. This species is an established model system in the study of the genetics, evolution, and ecology of heterostylous floral polymorphisms. Despite the long history of research focused on this and related species, the continued development of this system has been restricted due the absence of genomic and transcriptomic resources.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Results</h3><p>We present here a <i>de novo</i> draft genome assembly of <i>P. veris</i> covering 301.8 Mb, or approximately 63% of the estimated 479.22 Mb genome, with an N50 contig size of 9.5 Kb, an N50 scaffold size of 164 Kb, and containing an estimated 19,507 genes. The results of a RADseq bulk segregant analysis allow for the confident identification of four genome scaffolds that are linked to the <i>P. veris S</i>-locus. RNAseq data from both <i>P. veris</i> and the closely related species <i>P. vulgaris</i> allow for the characterization of 113 candidate heterostyly genes that show significant floral morph-specific differential expression. One candidate gene of particular interest is a duplicated <i>GLOBOSA</i> homolog that may be unique to <i>Primula</i> (<i>PveGLO2</i>), and is completely silenced in L-morph flowers.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Conclusions</h3><p>The <i>P. veris</i> genome represents the first genome assembled from a heterostylous species, and thus provides an immensely important resource for future studies focused on the evolution and genetic dissection of heterostyly. As the first genome assembled from the Primulaceae, the <i>P. veris</i> genome will also facilitate the expanded application of phylogenomic methods in this diverse family and the eudicots as a whole.</p></div></div></section>
                    
    


                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Background"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Background</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>With over 350,000 described species, angiosperms currently represent the dominant, most diverse group of plants on earth [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Scheffers BR, Joppa LN, Pimm SL, Laurance WF (2012) What we know and don’t know about Earth's missing biodiversity. Trends Ecol Evol 27:501–10" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d17215e722">1</a>]. Their success has been frequently linked with the evolution of a complex structure, the flower, which typically includes both male and female sexual organs - often inconspicuous - inside whorls of attractive, asexual organs. This evolutionary innovation opened up a new landscape of opportunities for elaborate interactions with animals, mostly insects, which can transfer male gametes (that is, pollen grains) between flowers of different plants more efficiently than abiotic vectors (for example, wind; [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Grant V (1949) Pollination systems as isolating mechanisms in angiosperms. Evolution 3:82–97" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d17215e725">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Crane PR, Friis EM, Pedersen KR (1995) The origin and early diversification of angiosperms. Nature 374:27–33" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d17215e728">3</a>]). Most flowers are hermaphroditic, theoretically enabling fertilization within the same individual (selfing), a breeding system that can lead to detrimental evolutionary consequences [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Charlesworth D, Charlesworth B (1987) Inbreeding depression and its evolutionary consequences. Annu Rev Ecol Syst 18:237–68" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d17215e731">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Wright SI, Ness RW, Foxe JP, Barrett SCH (2008) Genomic consequences of outcrossing and selfing in plants. Int J Plant Sci 169:105–18" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d17215e734">5</a>]. Different strategies have thus evolved in flowering plants to avoid selfing and promote outcrossing, and one of the most effective mechanisms is heterostyly.</p><p>Extensively investigated in primroses (<i>Primula</i> L., Primulaceae) by Darwin [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Darwin C (1877) The Different Forms of Flowers on Plants of the Same Species. John Murray, London" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d17215e743">6</a>], heterostyly refers to a floral polymorphism whereby individuals in a population produce dissimilar types of flowers (two in some taxa, three in others) with male and female sexual organs in different, but spatially matching positions [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Barrett S (2002) The evolution of plant sexual diversity. Nat Rev Genet 3:274–84" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d17215e746">7</a>]. For example, in <i>Primula</i>, plants produce two types of flowers (that is, distyly): either long-styled flowers with anthers attached midway along the floral tube (‘L-morph’, or ‘pin’), or short-styled flowers with anthers attached at the top of the floral tube (‘S-morph’, or ‘thrum’). Male and female sexual organs of L-morph and S-morph flowers, respectively, are thus placed in complementary positions, a condition known as reciprocal herkogamy. Heterostylous species frequently employ a genetic mechanism for rejecting pollen of the same plant and pollen from the same floral morph known as heteromorphic self-incompatibility [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="McCubbin A. Heteromorphic self-incompatibility in Primula: twenty-first century tools promise to unravel a classic nineteenth century model system. 2008. p. 289–308." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d17215e752">8</a>]. The combination of reciprocal herkogamy and self-incompatibility enforces outcrossing [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Keller B, de Vos JM, Conti E (2012) Decrease of sexual organ reciprocity between heterostylous primrose species, with possible functional and evolutionary implications. Ann Bot 110:1233–44" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d17215e756">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Keller B, Thomson JD, Conti E (2014) Heterostyly promotes disassortative pollination and reduces sexual interference in Darwin’s primroses: evidence from experimental studies. Funct Ecol 28:1413–25" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d17215e759">10</a>], thus avoiding the potentially negative effects of inbreeding. The adaptive value of heterostyly may thus explain why this system has evolved repeatedly in the angiosperms, and is presently known to occur in at least 28 different families [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Barrett SCH (1992) Heterostylous genetic polymorphisms: model systems for evolutionary analysis. Monographs on Theoretical and Applied Genetics 15:1–29" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d17215e762">11</a>].</p><p>The best-studied group of heterostylous plants is arguably <i>Primula</i>, comprising 400 to 500 species, of which approximately 90% are distylous and 10% homostylous, the latter producing self-compatible flowers of only one type. Primroses occur primarily in temperate, alpine, and arctic habitats of the northern hemisphere, with the main center of species diversity in the Sino-Himalayan region and secondary centers in the major, circumboreal mountain chains [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Richards A (2003) Primula. Timber Press, Portland, OR" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d17215e771">12</a>]. Owing to its fascinating reproductive system, attractive flowers, and common occurrence in the historical centers of Western science, the genus has been intensively examined from comparative perspectives for at least 150 years. Numerous studies are thus available on phylogenetic relationships within the genus (for example, [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Mast A, Kelso S, Conti E (2006) Are any primroses (Primula) primitively monomorphic? New Phytol 171:605–16" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d17215e774">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="de Vos JM, Hughes CE, Schneeweiss GM, Moore BR, Conti E (2014) Heterostyly accelerates diversification via reduced extinction in primroses. P R Soc B 281:20140075–5" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d17215e777">14</a>]), biogeography, ecology, and variation of ploidy levels (for example, [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Guggisberg A, Mansion G, Kelso S, Conti E (2006) Evolution of biogeographic patterns, ploidy levels, and breeding systems in a diploid-polyploid species complex of Primula. New Phytol 171:617–32" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d17215e780">15</a>-<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Schorr G, Pearman PB, Guisan A, Kadereit JW (2013) Combining palaeodistribution modelling and phylogeographical approaches for identifying glacial refugia in Alpine Primula. J Biogeogr 40:1947–60" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d17215e784">19</a>]), floral and reproductive biology (for example, [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Darwin C (1877) The Different Forms of Flowers on Plants of the Same Species. John Murray, London" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d17215e787">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Keller B, de Vos JM, Conti E (2012) Decrease of sexual organ reciprocity between heterostylous primrose species, with possible functional and evolutionary implications. Ann Bot 110:1233–44" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d17215e790">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Keller B, Thomson JD, Conti E (2014) Heterostyly promotes disassortative pollination and reduces sexual interference in Darwin’s primroses: evidence from experimental studies. Funct Ecol 28:1413–25" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d17215e793">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="de Vos JM, Keller B, Isham ST, Kelso S, Conti E (2012) Reproductive implications of herkogamy in homostylous primroses: variation during anthesis and reproductive assurance in alpine environments. Funct Ecol 26:854–65" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d17215e796">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Nishihiro J, Washitani I, Thomson J, Thomson B (2000) Patterns and consequences of stigma height variation in a natural population of a distylous plant. Primula sieboldii. Funct Ecol 14:502–12" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d17215e799">21</a>]), genetics of heterostyly (for example, [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Dowrick VPJ (1956) Heterostyly and homostyly in Primula obconica. Heredity 10:219–36" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d17215e803">22</a>-<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Wedderburn F, Richards A (1992) Secondary homostyly in Primula L - evidence for the model of the S-supergene. New Phytol 121:649–55" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d17215e806">25</a>]), evolution of breeding systems (13, for example, [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Ernst A. Stammesgeschicktliche Untersuchungen zum Heterostylie-Problem - 7. Stand des Nachweises monomorpher Arten, homostyler Sippen und anderer genetisch bedingter Abweichungen vom “klassischen” Blütendimorphismus in den Sektionen der Gattung Primula. Archiv der Julius Klaus-Stiftung. 1962;XXXVII:1–127." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d17215e809">26</a>-<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="de Vos JM, Wüest RO, Conti E (2014) Small and ugly? Phylogenetic analyses of the “selfing syndrome” reveal complex evolutionary fates of monomorphic primrose flowers. Evolution 68:1042–57" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d17215e812">28</a>]), and conservation ecology and genetics (for example, [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Jiménez A, Mansour H, Keller B, Conti E (2013) Low genetic diversity and high levels of inbreeding in the Sinai primrose (Primula boveana), a species on the brink of extinction. Plant Syst Evol 300:1199–208" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d17215e815">29</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Ishihama F, Ueno S, Tsumura Y, Washitani I (2006) Effects of density and floral morph on pollen flow and seed reproduction of an endangered heterostylous herb. Primula sieboldii. J Ecol 94:846–55" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d17215e818">30</a>]). Furthermore, primroses also represent one of the most popular garden plants in Europe, Japan, and North America, valued as spring-blooming perennials and rock-garden alpines. Primroses are prominent in the horticultural trade, and business associated with the sale of plants, organization of exhibitions, and collecting expeditions is estimated at 50 million USD annually (J. Richards, personal communication).</p><p>Within the genus, <i>Primula</i> section <i>Primula</i> has received the most scientific attention, starting with Darwin’s [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Darwin C (1877) The Different Forms of Flowers on Plants of the Same Species. John Murray, London" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d17215e830">6</a>] seminal work on the floral morphology and reproductive biology of <i>Primula veris</i> (cowslip), <i>Primula vulgaris</i> (primrose), and <i>Primula elatior</i> (oxlip). The section includes six distylous and one homostylous species, all diploids with a base chromosome number of 11 (that is, 2n = 2x = 22). Typical elements of the spring flora in many parts of Eurasia, these three species are the most widespread in the section, ranging from Western Europe to central and even far-Eastern Asia [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Schmidt-Lebuhn AN, de Vos JM, Keller B, Conti E (2012) Phylogenetic analysis of Primula section Primula reveals rampant non-monophyly among morphologically distinct species. Mol Phylogenet Evol 65:23–34" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d17215e843">31</a>]. Their abundance and easy accessibility in Europe may partially explain why they have been investigated so intensively. A broad range of studies have been performed on reproductive isolation and hybridization among these three species ([<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Keller B, de Vos JM, Conti E (2012) Decrease of sexual organ reciprocity between heterostylous primrose species, with possible functional and evolutionary implications. Ann Bot 110:1233–44" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d17215e846">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Keller B, Thomson JD, Conti E (2014) Heterostyly promotes disassortative pollination and reduces sexual interference in Darwin’s primroses: evidence from experimental studies. Funct Ecol 28:1413–25" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d17215e849">10</a>], for example, [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Valentine D (1947) Studies in British Primulas. I. Hybridization between primrose and oxlip (Primula vulgaris Huds. and P. elatior Schreb.). New Phytol 46:229–53" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d17215e852">32</a>-<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Jacquemyn H, Vandepitte K, Roldan-Ruiz I, Honnay O (2009) Rapid loss of genetic variation in a founding population of Primula elatior (Primulaceae) after colonization. Ann Bot 103:777–83" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d17215e855">41</a>]), pollination biology, ecology and conservation (for example, [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Brys R, Jacquemyn H, Endels P, Van Rossum F, Hermy M, Triest L et al (2004) Reduced reproductive success in small populations of the self-incompatible Primula vulgaris. J Ecol 92:5–14" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d17215e859">42</a>-<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Van Rossum F, Triest L (2006) Within-population genetic variation in the distylous Primula veris: Does floral morph anisoplethy matter in fragmented habitats? Perspectives in Plant Ecology. Evolution and Systematics 7:263–73" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d17215e862">46</a>]), floral morphology, self-incompatibility, and the genetics of distyly (for example, [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Webster M, Gilmartin P (2003) A comparison of early floral ontogeny in wild-type and floral homeotic mutant phenotypes of Primula. Planta 216:903–17" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d17215e865">47</a>-<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Heslop-Harrison Y, Heslop-Harrison J, Shivanna K (1981) Heterostyly in Primula. 1. Fine-structural and cytochemical features of the stigma and style in Primula vulgaris Huds. Protoplasma 107:171–87" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d17215e868">52</a>]).</p><p>The genus, and in particular <i>Primula</i> sect. <i>Primula</i>, have thus emerged as a model for a broad spectrum of evolutionary, ecological, and conservation studies, and as the paradigm for exploring the genetic control of distyly [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Barrett S (2002) The evolution of plant sexual diversity. Nat Rev Genet 3:274–84" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d17215e881">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Cohen J (2010) “A case to which no parallel exists”: The influence of Darwin’s different forms of flowers. Am J Bot 97:701–16" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d17215e884">53</a>]. Classic genetic studies on primroses, based on manual crosses and segregation analyses, established that distyly is governed by a single Mendelian locus (the <i>S</i>-locus), with long-styled and short-styled plants being homozygous (<i>ss</i>) and heterozygous (<i>Ss</i>), respectively, and the homozygous dominant genotype (<i>SS</i>) being lethal [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Bateson W, Gregory R (1905) On the inheritance of heterostylism in Primula. Proceedings of the Royal Society of London Series B, Containing Papers of a Biological Character 76:581–6" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d17215e900">54</a>-<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Mather K (1950) The genetical architecture of heterostyly in Primula sinensis. Evolution 4:340–52" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d17215e903">57</a>]. A minimum of three genes are thought to be tightly linked at the <i>S</i>-locus (sometimes called the S-supergene), controlling the length of the style (<i>Gg</i>: short; <i>gg</i>: long), the position of the anthers (<i>Aa</i>: high; <i>aa</i>: low), the size of pollen grains (<i>Pp</i>: large; <i>pp</i>: small) and other ancillary traits ([<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Lewis D, Jones DA (1992) The genetics of heterostyly. In: Barrett SCH (ed) Evolution and function of heterostyly. Springer-Verlag, New York, pp 129–50" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d17215e929">23</a>], for example, the length of the stigmatic papillae [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Kurian V, Richards A (1997) A new recombinant in the heteromorphy “S” supergene in Primula. Heredity 78:383–90" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d17215e932">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Barrett S, Shore J (2008) New insights on heterostyly: comparative biology, ecology and genetics. In: Franklin-Tong VE (ed) Self-incompatibility in flowering plants. Springer, New York, pp 3–32" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d17215e935">58</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Schwander T, Libbrecht R, Keller L (2014) Supergenes and complex phenotypes. Curr Biol 24:R288–94" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d17215e938">59</a>]). Large populations often harbor individuals with intermediate phenotypes suggestive of rare recombination events at the <i>S</i>-locus. Lewis and Jones [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Lewis D, Jones DA (1992) The genetics of heterostyly. In: Barrett SCH (ed) Evolution and function of heterostyly. Springer-Verlag, New York, pp 129–50" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d17215e944">23</a>] used the frequency of such recombinants to infer the linkage order of the three loci as <i>GPA</i> in <i>Primula</i> subgenus <i>Auriculastrum</i>, but Kurian and Richards [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Kurian V, Richards A (1997) A new recombinant in the heteromorphy “S” supergene in Primula. Heredity 78:383–90" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d17215e957">24</a>] found that the gene order was more likely <i>GAP</i> in <i>Primula</i> section <i>Primula</i>. Beyond the relative order of the three linked loci, little is known regarding the absolute size of the <i>Primula S</i>-locus, as well as the number and function of specific genes that these loci represent [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="McCubbin A. Heteromorphic self-incompatibility in Primula: twenty-first century tools promise to unravel a classic nineteenth century model system. 2008. p. 289–308." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d17215e973">8</a>]. To date, just three genes have been identified as potentially linked to the <i>Primula S</i>-locus, and none of these have been proposed as functionally integral to the differential development of L- and S-morph flowers. In 2007, Li <i>et al.</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Li J, Webster M, Dudas B, Cook H, Manfield I, Davies B et al (2008) The S locus‐linked Primula homeotic mutant sepaloid shows characteristics of a B‐function mutant but does not result from mutation in a B2 function gene. Plant J 56:1–12" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d17215e982">60</a>] employed fluorescent differential display to identify, clone, and sequence L- and S-morph alleles of two genes that they named <i>PvSLL1</i> and <i>PvSLL2</i>, which represent an unknown plasma membrane protein, and a gene with sequence similarity to <i>Arabidopsis</i> flower-timing genes <i>Col9</i> and <i>Col10</i>, respectively. While searching for the genes responsible for the <i>S</i>-locus-linked floral homeotic mutant <i>sepaloid</i> in <i>Primula vulgaris</i>, Li <i>et al.</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Li J, Webster M, Dudas B, Cook H, Manfield I, Davies B et al (2008) The S locus‐linked Primula homeotic mutant sepaloid shows characteristics of a B‐function mutant but does not result from mutation in a B2 function gene. Plant J 56:1–12" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d17215e1014">60</a>] identified <i>PvGLO</i>, a homolog to the B-function MADS-box gene <i>GLOBOSA</i>. While their results suggested that <i>PvGLO</i> was not likely responsible for the mutant <i>sepaloid</i> phenotype, the gene appeared to carry alleles exhibiting morph-specific segregation, and it was thus assumed to be linked to the <i>Primula S</i>-locus.</p><p>Given that the <i>Primula S</i>-locus is largely shielded from recombination, there are certain well-established theoretical predictions that can be made regarding the genome sequence harboring the <i>S</i>-locus. The classic model of Muller’s Ratchet predicts that reduced recombination will lead to the fixation of a large number of linked sites on both L-morph and S-morph <i>S</i>-locus haplotypes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Muller H (1964) The relation of recombination to mutational advance. Mutat Res 1:2–9" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d17215e1045">61</a>]. This implies that both coding and non-coding sequence residing within the boundaries of the <i>S</i>-locus should exhibit elevated heterozygosity in S-morph (<i>Ss</i>) plants relative to L-morph plants (<i>ss</i>).</p><p>Despite the plethora of evolutionary, ecological, morphological, and genetic studies on <i>Primula</i> and its longstanding value as a prime biological study system, we still lack the genomic resources that would allow us to perform detailed analyses of speciation processes, identify areas of the genome that are more porous to introgression, characterize the genetic basis of adaptation to alpine/arctic habitats, exploit the genes that control traits of special horticultural value, and finally elucidate the enduring mystery of the molecular basis of distyly. In turn, the molecular characterization of the <i>S</i>-locus would open up new avenues of research into the evolutionary pathways leading to heterostyly [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Mast A, Conti E (2006) The primrose path to heterostyly. New Phytol 171:439–42" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d17215e1067">27</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Lloyd DG (1992) The evolution of heterostyly. In: Barrett SCH (ed) Evolution and function of heterostyly. Springer, Berlin, pp 151–78" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d17215e1070">62</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Charlesworth D, Charlesworth B (1979) A model for the evolution of distyly. Am Nat 114:467–98" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d17215e1073">63</a>] and the repeated evolution and loss of this trait in angiosperms [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="de Vos JM, Hughes CE, Schneeweiss GM, Moore BR, Conti E (2014) Heterostyly accelerates diversification via reduced extinction in primroses. P R Soc B 281:20140075–5" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d17215e1077">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="de Vos JM, Wüest RO, Conti E (2014) Small and ugly? Phylogenetic analyses of the “selfing syndrome” reveal complex evolutionary fates of monomorphic primrose flowers. Evolution 68:1042–57" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d17215e1080">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Shore J, Arbo M, Fernandez A (2006) Breeding system variation, genetics and evolution in the Turneraceae. New Phytol 171:539–51" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d17215e1083">64</a>]. Here, we present the first draft genome assembly of a heterostylous species, focusing on <i>Primula veris</i>, because this species has long been a favorite target of scientific studies (as reviewed above) and represents a well-defined evolutionary lineage [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Schmidt-Lebuhn AN, de Vos JM, Keller B, Conti E (2012) Phylogenetic analysis of Primula section Primula reveals rampant non-monophyly among morphologically distinct species. Mol Phylogenet Evol 65:23–34" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d17215e1089">31</a>]. This species is diploid with a chromosome number of 2n = 22 and estimated DNA content of 479.22 Mb [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Siljak-Yakovlev S, Pustahija F, Bogunić F, Šolić EM, Muratović E, Bašić N et al (2010) Towards a genome size and chromosome number database of Balkan flora: C-values in 343 taxa with novel values for 242. Adv Sci Lett 3:190–213" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d17215e1092">65</a>]. Our aim in assembling the <i>P. veris</i> genome is primarily to develop genomic resources for this species and the entire genus <i>Primula</i>, to identify genome scaffolds that segregate with the <i>P. veris S</i>-locus, and to use these newly created resources to examine genes previously identified as linked to the <i>Primula S</i>-locus and characterize novel candidate distyly genes. Our genome and transcriptome assemblies enable the continued development of <i>P. veris</i> as a model system to study the genetic components of the <i>S</i>-locus and the evolution and maintenance of reproductive barriers within <i>Primula</i> sect. <i>Primula</i>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results and discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results and discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome and transcriptome assemblies</h3><p>We generated a large amount of sequence data from a diverse suite of sequencing libraries to assemble the draft <i>P. veris</i> genome. A full account of our sequencing efforts is shown in Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#Tab1">1</a>. Using these data, we employed a two-step strategy for <i>de novo</i> genome assembly in order to fully leverage the long-read data generated by PacBio RS. Our first assembly was performed using only short-read (that is, 100 to 250 bp) sequences generated from standard paired-end and 3 to 9 Kb mate-pair libraries on Illumina HiSeq, MiSeq, and Ion Proton platforms. This assembly was based on a total of 54.5 Gb of raw data and resulted in a total of 48,812 contigs that were grouped into 9,002 unique scaffolds (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S1). The total contig length is 232.2 Mb and the total scaffold length, including gaps, is 301.8 Mb, which represent 49% and 63%, respectively, of the estimated 479.22 Mb <i>P. veris</i> genome [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Siljak-Yakovlev S, Pustahija F, Bogunić F, Šolić EM, Muratović E, Bašić N et al (2010) Towards a genome size and chromosome number database of Balkan flora: C-values in 343 taxa with novel values for 242. Adv Sci Lett 3:190–213" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d17215e1149">65</a>]. The N50 contig size is 9.5 Kb, and the N50 scaffold size including gaps is 164 Kb, with a median gap size of 960 bp. The largest and smallest scaffolds are 2.14 Mb and 888 bp, respectively.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 
                          <b>Summary of sequence data used in the</b>
                          <b><i>de novo</i></b>
                          <b>assembly of the draft</b>
                          <b><i>P. veris</i></b>
                          <b>genome</b>
                        </b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                <p>The transcriptome sequencing of <i>P. veris</i> and <i>P. vulgaris</i> yielded more than 200 million paired reads, which were distributed quite evenly among the six RNAseq libraries that we multiplexed and sequenced in parallel on a single Illumina flow cell (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S2). Our <i>de novo P. veris</i> transcriptome assembly contains 25,409 putatively unique transcripts, and the <i>P. vulgaris</i> transcriptome assembly contains 24,318 unique transcripts. We also performed <i>de novo</i> transcriptome assemblies of the related species <i>P. obconica</i>, <i>P. wilsonii</i>, and <i>P. poissonii</i> using data available on the Genbank Sequence Read Archive (SRA). Our <i>de novo</i> transcriptome assembly of <i>P. obconica</i> was found to contain 22,752 transcripts, but the transcriptome assemblies of <i>P. poissonii</i> and <i>P. wilsonii</i> were considerably smaller, with 11,905 and 12,927 unique transcripts, respectively. This reduced transcript pool likely represents the fact that the publicly available Genbank SRA data for these species contained far fewer raw sequence reads [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Zhang L, Yan H-F, Wu W, Yu H, Ge X-J (2013) Comparative transcriptome analysis and marker development of two closely related Primrose species (Primula poissonii and Primula wilsonii). BMC Genomics 14:329" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d17215e1811">66</a>] than those we produced for <i>P. veris</i> and <i>P. vulgaris</i>, and than the reads available in the SRA for <i>P. obconica</i>.</p><p>To improve our <i>P. veris</i> genome assembly, we generated 3.4 Gb of PacBio RS sequence data with an average read length of 3,658 and an average base quality of 0.834 (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S1). These data, which contribute an additional 8× coverage for the <i>P. veris</i> genome, were integrated into our assembly through the implementation of the PbJelly software tool [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="English AC, Richards S, Han Y, Wang M, Vee V, Qu J et al (2012) Mind the gap: upgrading genomes with Pacific biosciences RS long-read sequencing technology. PLoS One 7:e47768" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d17215e1835">67</a>]. The PBJelly algorithm essentially anchors the long PacBio reads to the existing contigs and scaffolds and extends them partially spanning the contiguous gaps. When PacBio sequences are long enough to be anchored to both sides of a gap, then the gap is fully closed. Adding the PacBio RS data to the assembly resulted in a total of 21.15% of the gaps in the previous assembly being entirely closed and 38.4% of the ambiguous positions in the gaps being filled. This translated into a significant improvement of both the compactness and completeness of our <i>de novo P. veris</i> genome. At 269.7 Mb, the new total contig length increased by 19.4%; the new total scaffold length (including gaps) reached 310.07 Mb, a 2.7% increase over the draft assembly based on the Illumina and Ion Proton data alone. In terms of genome completeness, these contig-length numbers represent 56.3% and 64.7%, respectively, of the <i>P. veris</i> genome. The updated draft is also more compact: the number of contigs was reduced, by 16.88%, to 40,569 contigs, grouped into 8,764 unique scaffolds (2.65% fewer scaffolds, see Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S1). At 13.3 Kb, the N50 contig size grew by 40%, whereas the N50 scaffold size including gaps reached 165.9 Kb, with a median gap size of 664 bp. The largest and smallest contigs are 233,407 bp and 19 bp, respectively. The sizes of the largest and smallest scaffolds remained 2.14 Mb and 888 bp, respectively; this is not surprising, since gap-closing tends to bridge scaffolds of low to medium size. The assembly scaffolds on average contain 29 ambiguities per 10 kb. The GC content of the scaffolds, excluding gaps, is 33%, which is similar to the genome sequences of tomato (34%; [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Tomato Genome Consortium (2012) The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature 485:635–41" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d17215e1848">68</a>]) and kiwifruit (35.2%; [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Huang S, Ding J, Deng D, Tang W, Sun H, Liu D et al (2013) Draft genome of the kiwifruit Actinidia chinensis. Nat Commun 4:1–9" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d17215e1851">69</a>]).</p><p>Although the success of a draft genome assembly is strongly dependent on the genetic complexity of the specific organism and its genome size, we find the overall quality of the draft <i>P. veris</i> assembly, based on the aforementioned measures, to be consistent with the majority of <i>de novo</i> genome assembly efforts reported in the last few years. Unsurprisingly, genome assemblies generated from highly homozygous samples, such as the PN40024 grapevine line [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Jaillon O, Aury J-M, Noel B, Policriti A, Clepet C, Casagrande A et al (2007) The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449:463–7" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d17215e1863">70</a>], or those employing a larger number of <i>ad hoc</i> mate pair libraries (for example, domesticated apple <i>Malus</i> × <i>domestica</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Velasco R, Zharkikh A, Affourtit J, Dhingra A, Cestaro A, Kalyanaraman A et al (2010) The genome of the domesticated apple (Malus × domestica Borkh.). Nat Genet 42:833–9" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d17215e1876">71</a>]; kiwifruit <i>Actinidia chinensis</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Huang S, Ding J, Deng D, Tang W, Sun H, Liu D et al (2013) Draft genome of the kiwifruit Actinidia chinensis. Nat Commun 4:1–9" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d17215e1882">69</a>]; mulberry <i>Morus notabilis</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="He N, Zhang C, Qi X, Zhao S, Tao Y, Yang G et al (2013) Draft genome sequence of the mulberry tree Morus notabilis. Nat Commun 4:1–9" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d17215e1888">72</a>]) resulted in higher quality drafts exhibiting a three- to four-fold decrease in the number of scaffolds and a two- to four-fold increase in the corresponding N50, relative to our assembly. However, when compared to assemblies in which similar resources were used, our assembly is quite compact. For example, our <i>P. veris</i> assembly is between two and 10 times more compact than the draft genomes of <i>Cannabis sativa</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="van Bakel H, Stout JM, Cote AG, Tallon CM, Sharpe AG, Hughes TR et al (2011) The draft genome and transcriptome of Cannabis sativa. Genome Biol 12:R102" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d17215e1898">73</a>] and European pear (<i>Pyrus communis</i> ‘Bartlett’ [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Chagné D, Crowhurst RN, Pindo M, Thrimawithana A, Deng C, Ireland H et al (2014) The draft genome sequence of European pear (Pyrus communis L. “Bartlett”). PLoS One 9:e92644" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d17215e1904">74</a>]), which contain 136,290 and 142,083 scaffolds, respectively, and scaffold N50s smaller than 90 Kb. These genome projects employed two mate pair libraries of sizes similar to our study, but it is important to point out that their minimum contig size was less than 500 bp, while our assembly is based on a minimum contig size of 1,000 bp. Our assembly is one of the first to specifically employ PacBio sequence data to fill scaffold gaps in a heterozygous plant genome, and this aspect, together with the use of a third, larger mate pair library, may be responsible for the improved compactness (that is, fewer contigs) of our assembly.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Genome annotation and quality assessment</h3><p>Of the 8,764 scaffolds that make up our genome assembly, 2,495 (28.5%) were found to contain annotated genes. Our final annotation of the <i>P. veris</i> genome contained a total of 19,507 predicted genes, less than the number of genes found in the relatively well-annotated genomes of <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> (27,029 genes; [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Swarbreck D, Wilks C, Lamesch P, Berardini TZ, Garcia-Hernandez M, Foerster H et al (2007) The Arabidopsis Information Resource (TAIR): gene structure and function annotation. Nucleic Acids Res 36:D1009–14" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d17215e1921">75</a>]), sacred lotus (<i>Nelumbo nucifera</i>, 26,685 genes; [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Ming R, VanBuren R, Liu Y, Yang M, Han Y, Li L-T et al (2013) Genome of the long-living sacred lotus (Nelumbo nucifera Gaertn.). Genome Biol 14:R41" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d17215e1927">76</a>]), and mulberry (29,338 genes; [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="He N, Zhang C, Qi X, Zhao S, Tao Y, Yang G et al (2013) Draft genome sequence of the mulberry tree Morus notabilis. Nat Commun 4:1–9" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d17215e1931">72</a>]). Furthermore, we find about 7,000 fewer genes predicted in the genome annotation compared with the <i>de novo P. veris</i> transcriptome assembly (25,409 predicted genes; see above), but most likely the real number of genes is somewhere in between those two estimates, and a combination of factors might have contributed to underestimating the predictions in the genome annotation and inflating the number of genes predicted in the <i>de novo</i> transcriptome assembly. First, transcripts predicted by the <i>de novo</i> transcriptome assembly are based on only one source of evidence, that is, the RNA-seq data, and represent only one source of evidence in the Maker2 genome annotation process. For a transcript to be reported by Maker2, at least two sources of evidence are required, hence those transcripts that are not strongly supported by a second source of evidence, such as <i>ab initio</i> gene prediction algorithms or protein homology are not reported in the final genome annotation. Additionally, a <i>de novo</i> transcriptome assembly alone is often not able to completely discriminate between multiple isoforms of a specific gene, which may slightly inflate the estimated number of genes based on these data alone. The predicted gene content of <i>P. veris</i> is significantly reduced compared to kiwifruit (39,040 genes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Huang S, Ding J, Deng D, Tang W, Sun H, Liu D et al (2013) Draft genome of the kiwifruit Actinidia chinensis. Nat Commun 4:1–9" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d17215e1953">69</a>]) and European pear (43,413 genes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Chagné D, Crowhurst RN, Pindo M, Thrimawithana A, Deng C, Ireland H et al (2014) The draft genome sequence of European pear (Pyrus communis L. “Bartlett”). PLoS One 9:e92644" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d17215e1956">74</a>]), probably owing to the relatively recent whole genome duplications in the latter two species. The Maker2 pipeline identified a total of 279,271 repetitive elements representing approximately 7% of the <i>P. veris</i> draft genome assembly (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S3).</p><p>To assess the completeness of the final genome assembly, we searched for the presence of 248 conserved core eukaryotic genes using the CEGMA software package (CEGs; [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Parra G, Bradnam K, Korf I (2007) CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes. Bioinformatics 23:1061–7" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d17215e1968">77</a>]). We found confident hits to 198 (79.84%) full length (that is, &gt;70% alignment) CEG proteins and 234 partial (94.35%) CEG proteins, where partial matches are defined by the CEGMA default pre-computed minimum alignment score for each CEG [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Parra G, Bradnam K, Korf I (2007) CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes. Bioinformatics 23:1061–7" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d17215e1971">77</a>]. These results suggest an exceptional level of completeness for this first draft genome sequence of a heterozygous, non-model plant. Annotated scaffolds contained a median number of four genes, and the largest scaffold (Contig0; 2.15 Mb) was found to contain 352 predicted genes, and generally, longer scaffolds tend to contain more genes. The quality of the gene prediction and annotation was evaluated in a number of ways. Searching the predicted gene set with the InterProScan tool, we found that 15,659 (85.6%) of predicted proteins contain PFAM domains. One commonly applied quality metric of genome annotations is the cumulative annotation edit distance (AED), which represents the agreement between predicted gene models and external evidence. A gene model with an AED score of zero indicates complete agreement between the predicted gene model and, for example, a transcript from the <i>de novo P. veris</i> transcriptome assembly, which we incorporate as external evidence in the Maker2 pipeline [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Holt C, Yandell M (2011) MAKER2: an annotation pipeline and genome-database management tool for second-generation genome projects. BMC Bioinformatics 12:491" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d17215e1977">78</a>]. As the <i>ab initio</i> gene predictors are trained through iterative Maker2 runs, the cumulative fraction of predicted gene models with low AED scores increases, thus indicating more agreement between gene models and external evidence (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#MOESM3">3</a>: Figure S2). In our final annotation, approximately 80% of gene models have an AED score of 0.2 or less, indicative of a high degree of agreement between predicted gene models and external evidence.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Comparative transcriptome analyses</h3><p>We used the OrthoMCL pipeline [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Li L (2003) OrthoMCL: Identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res 13:2178–89" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d17215e1995">79</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 80" title="Fischer S, Brunk BP, Chen F, Gao X, Harb OS, Iodice JB et al (2011) Using OrthoMCL to assign proteins to OrthoMCL-DB groups or to cluster proteomes into new ortholog groups. John Wiley &amp; Sons, Inc., Hoboken, NJ" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR80" id="ref-link-section-d17215e1998">80</a>] to identify sets of putatively orthologous loci at two phylogenetic scales: (1) among transcriptomes of five species of <i>Primula</i>; and (2) among <i>P. veris</i> and four Euasteridae species with published genome assemblies (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#Fig1">1</a>). Our comparative <i>Primula</i> transcriptome analyses confidently identified a total of 4,207 orthologs among the transcriptomes of <i>P. veris</i>, <i>P. vulgaris</i>, <i>P. obconica, P. wilsonii</i>, and <i>P. poissonii</i>, with 2,391 of these likely representing single-copy genes in all of the species (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#Fig1">1</a>A; see Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#MOESM4">4</a>). The most recent phylogenetic hypotheses for the genus <i>Primula</i> suggest that <i>P. veris</i>, <i>P. vulgaris</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Schmidt-Lebuhn AN, de Vos JM, Keller B, Conti E (2012) Phylogenetic analysis of Primula section Primula reveals rampant non-monophyly among morphologically distinct species. Mol Phylogenet Evol 65:23–34" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d17215e2042">31</a>] and <i>P. poissonii</i>, <i>P. wilsonii</i>, respectively, belong to two different sister clades, [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Zhang L, Yan H-F, Wu W, Yu H, Ge X-J (2013) Comparative transcriptome analysis and marker development of two closely related Primrose species (Primula poissonii and Primula wilsonii). BMC Genomics 14:329" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d17215e2052">66</a>], with <i>P. obconica</i> included in a third clade basal to the other two clades [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Mast A, Kelso S, Conti E (2006) Are any primroses (Primula) primitively monomorphic? New Phytol 171:605–16" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d17215e2058">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="de Vos JM, Hughes CE, Schneeweiss GM, Moore BR, Conti E (2014) Heterostyly accelerates diversification via reduced extinction in primroses. P R Soc B 281:20140075–5" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d17215e2061">14</a>]. Our results are consistent with these hypothesized phylogenetic relationships, for we find more shared orthologs between <i>P. vulgaris</i> and <i>P. veris</i> than in all other pairwise comparisons (3,205), and a similar number of putatively orthologous genes limited to <i>P. veris</i>, <i>P. vulgaris</i>, and <i>P. obconica</i> (3,211). However, we do not see a similar number of shared orthologs unique to the sister species <i>P. poissonii</i> and <i>P. wilsonii</i> (487), but this result might be explained by the fact that these transcriptomes were assembled with far less sequence data [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Zhang L, Yan H-F, Wu W, Yu H, Ge X-J (2013) Comparative transcriptome analysis and marker development of two closely related Primrose species (Primula poissonii and Primula wilsonii). BMC Genomics 14:329" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d17215e2087">66</a>] than our <i>de novo</i> transcriptome assemblies, and thus they are likely to represent a smaller proportion of the expressed genes. Our comparative analyses of euasterid transcriptomes identified a total of 8,079 putatively orthologous genes, and of these approximately 2,402 are likely to be single copy in all examined species (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#Fig1">1</a>B; see Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#MOESM5">5</a>). These orthologous gene sets provide a framework for the development of future phylogenomic studies aimed at resolving species-level relationships within the genus <i>Primula</i>, or deeper phylogenetic relationships within the Euasteridae.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Figure 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-014-0567-z/MediaObjects/13059_2014_567_Fig1_HTML.gif?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-014-0567-z/MediaObjects/13059_2014_567_Fig1_HTML.gif" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>
                          <b>Venn diagrams of orthologous gene clusters.</b> The number of putatively unique transcripts in each transcriptome is shown below the taxon name. The value in parentheses represents the number of orthologous genes that are most likely to be single-copy in all of the five transcriptomes sampled. <b>(A)</b> Orthologous groups identified in comparative transcriptome analysis of five species of <i>Primula</i>. <b>(B)</b> Orthologous groups identified in comparative transcriptome analysis of five Euasterid species with draft genome assemblies. See also Additional files <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#MOESM4">4</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#MOESM5">5</a>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Gene expression differences between floral morphs and functional analysis of candidate genes</h3><p>To search for genes that might underlie the phenotypic differences between the floral morphs in <i>Primula</i>, we compared gene expression between flowers of L- and S-morph plants in both <i>P. vulgaris</i> and <i>P. veris</i>. Given that we sampled RNA from floral bud tissues 3 to 5 days prior to anthesis, our results are primarily relevant to the later stage of floral development. At this stage, cell growth in the style and in the corolla tube below the point of stamen attachment differs between L- and S-morph flowers. Differential cell growth is thought to be primarily responsible for differences in style length and the relative position of the anthers in the middle or the top of the corolla tube, respectively [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Webster M, Gilmartin P (2006) Analysis of late stage flower development in Primula vulgaris reveals novel differences in cell morphology and temporal aspects of floral heteromorphy. New Phytol 171:591–603" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d17215e2157">48</a>]. We adopted a Benjamini-Hochberg false discovery rate (FDR) at 5% level to correct for multiple testing. At an FDR-adjusted <i>P</i> value threshold of 0.05, we found 620 and 677 genes in <i>P. veris</i> and <i>P. vulgaris</i>, respectively, that exhibited significant differential expression between morphs. To reduce the number of false positives in this gene set, we retained only the 113 genes that exhibit morph-specific differential expression in both <i>P. veris</i> and <i>P. vulgaris</i> floral buds (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S4). Of these 113 genes, the majority (that is, 73 genes) showed increased expression in S-morph versus L-morph floral buds. Because the distylous phenotype is limited to floral tissues, it is likely that genes involved in determining the floral morph would not be differentially expressed in L- and S-morph leaf tissues. We find that, of these 113 genes showing floral morph-specific differential expression, 69 are not differentially expressed in <i>P. veris</i> L- and S-morph leaves (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S4).</p><p>We examined the candidate set of 113 differentially expressed genes to determine the types of functional gene classes represented. Functional annotations for 92 of the 113 genes were clustered using the DAVID bioinformatics resources, while the remaining 21 genes contained no conserved protein domains and are not readily assignable to functional classes. Annotation clusters represent groups of functionally similar GO terms associated with the gene list, thus providing a more direct biological interpretation of related terms for large gene lists [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 81" title="Huang DW, Sherman BT, Lempicki RA (2008) Systematic and integrative analysis of large gene lists using DAVID bioinformatics resources. Nat Protoc 4:44–57" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR81" id="ref-link-section-d17215e2189">81</a>]. As seen in Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#Fig2">2</a>, GO terms attributed to the 92 genes showing morph-specific differential expression can be grouped into 17 annotation clusters. More than 40% of the candidate genes involved in differential floral morph expression contain GO terms attributable to annotation cluster 1, which is characterized by the terms ‘extracellular’, ‘secreted’, and ‘hydrolase’, among others (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S5). These terms could be related to a wide array of biological processes, but it is plausible that some of the genes might control mechanisms of heteromorphic self-incompatibility typical of distylous <i>Primula</i> species. Self- and intra-morph pollen tube inhibition in <i>Primula</i> is thought to result from biochemical interactions between the pollen grain or pollen tube and tissues of the stigma and/or style [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 82" title="Richards A, Ibrahim H (1982) The breeding system in Primula veris L. II Pollen tube growth and seed-set. New Phytol 90:305–14" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR82" id="ref-link-section-d17215e2205">82</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 83" title="Wedderburn F, Richards A (1990) Variation in within-morph incompatibility inhibition sites in heteromorphic Primula L. New Phytol 116:149–62" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR83" id="ref-link-section-d17215e2208">83</a>]. Such biochemical interactions are likely to occur in the extracellular matrix or at the stigmatic surface, thus requiring the active secretion of molecules involved in the heteromorphic self-incompatibility mechanism [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Heslop-Harrison Y, Heslop-Harrison J, Shivanna K (1981) Heterostyly in Primula. 1. Fine-structural and cytochemical features of the stigma and style in Primula vulgaris Huds. Protoplasma 107:171–87" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d17215e2211">52</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 84" title="Shivanna K, Heslop-Harrison J, Heslop-Harrison Y (1981) Heterostyly in Primula. 2. Sites of pollen inhibition, and effects of pistil constituents on compatible and incompatible pollen-tube growth. Protoplasma 107:319–37" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR84" id="ref-link-section-d17215e2214">84</a>-<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 87" title="Carraro L, Lombardo G, Gerola P (1996) Stylar peroxidases and heteromorphic incompatibility reactions in Primula acaulisHill («thrum» morph). Caryologia 49:101–12" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR87" id="ref-link-section-d17215e2217">87</a>].</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Figure 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-014-0567-z/MediaObjects/13059_2014_567_Fig2_HTML.gif?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-014-0567-z/MediaObjects/13059_2014_567_Fig2_HTML.gif" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>
                          <b>Distribution of genes differentially expressed in L- and S-morph plants assigned to functional annotation clusters.</b> Annotation clusters are labeled with a single GO term that is representative of all of the terms defining cluster membership. See Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S5 for a complete listing of GO terms associated with each annotation cluster.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                <p>The remaining 16 annotation clusters contain GO terms associated with 25% to approximately 3% of the candidate genes; among these are a few clusters that stand out as being potentially relevant to the phenotypic differences associated with the two floral morphs. Specifically, annotation clusters 2 and 7 are characterized by GO terms associated with cell-wall organization and cell growth, respectively. These clusters are particularly interesting in the context of morph-specific development, because many of the phenotypic differences that define the L- and S-morph flowers involve differential cell proliferation and elongation in the corolla tube (the <i>A</i> gene) and the style (the <i>G</i> gene, [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Richards A (2003) Primula. Timber Press, Portland, OR" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d17215e2254">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Webster M, Gilmartin P (2006) Analysis of late stage flower development in Primula vulgaris reveals novel differences in cell morphology and temporal aspects of floral heteromorphy. New Phytol 171:591–603" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d17215e2257">48</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 88" title="Richards JH, Barrett S (1992) The development of heterostyly.
