In: Barrett SCH (ed) Evolution and function of heterostyly. Springer, New York, pp 85–127" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR88" id="ref-link-section-d17215e2260">88</a>]). Annotation cluster 15 is characterized by the terms ‘post-embryonic development’, ‘reproductive structure development’, and ‘reproductive developmental process’ (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S5). Floral development genes frequently act as transcription factors regulating the expression of a diverse suite of genes that influence the development of specific floral structures [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 89" title="Krizek BA, Meyerowitz EM (1996) The Arabidopsis homeotic genes APETALA3 and PISTILLATA are sufficient to provide the B class organ identity function. Development 122:11–22" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR89" id="ref-link-section-d17215e2267">89</a>]. It is thus possible that the genes associated with the GO terms of annotation cluster 15 could be coordinating the developmental changes associated with differential cell elongation observed in corolla tubes and styles of L- and S-morph plants.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">GLO1 &amp; GLO2: Duplicated GLOBOSA homologs in Primula</h3><p>One of the most intriguing results of our differential expression analysis in <i>P. veris</i> and <i>P. vulgaris</i> is the fact that BG8816696, the gene with the most significant morph-specific differential expression (as measured by FDR-adjusted <i>P</i> value; Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S4), is strikingly similar to the B-function MADS-box gene <i>GLOBOSA</i> (<i>E</i>-value = 0), which is a transcription factor integral in the normal development of petals and stamens in <i>Antirrhinum majus</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 90" title="Tröbner W, Ramirez L, Motte P, Hue I, Huijser P, Lönnig WE et al (1992) GLOBOSA: a homeotic gene which interacts with DEFICIENS in the control of Antirrhinum floral organogenesis. EMBO J 11:4693–704" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR90" id="ref-link-section-d17215e2301">90</a>]. Blast searches reveal that this gene had been previously sequenced from <i>P. vulgaris</i> by Li <i>et al.</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Li J, Webster M, Dudas B, Cook H, Manfield I, Davies B et al (2008) The S locus‐linked Primula homeotic mutant sepaloid shows characteristics of a B‐function mutant but does not result from mutation in a B2 function gene. Plant J 56:1–12" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d17215e2310">60</a>] and from <i>P. denticulata</i> by Viaene <i>et al.</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 91" title="Viaene T, Vekemans D, Irish VF, Geeraerts A, Huysmans S, Janssens S et al (2009) Pistillata–duplications as a mode for floral diversification in (Basal) asterids. Mol Biol Evol 26:2627–45" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR91" id="ref-link-section-d17215e2320">91</a>]. While Li <i>et al.</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Li J, Webster M, Dudas B, Cook H, Manfield I, Davies B et al (2008) The S locus‐linked Primula homeotic mutant sepaloid shows characteristics of a B‐function mutant but does not result from mutation in a B2 function gene. Plant J 56:1–12" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d17215e2326">60</a>] considered this gene to be single copy, Viaene <i>et al.</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 91" title="Viaene T, Vekemans D, Irish VF, Geeraerts A, Huysmans S, Janssens S et al (2009) Pistillata–duplications as a mode for floral diversification in (Basal) asterids. Mol Biol Evol 26:2627–45" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR91" id="ref-link-section-d17215e2333">91</a>] realized that there were actually two <i>GLOBOSA</i> homologs in <i>P. denticulata</i>. When we map the <i>GLOBOSA</i> homologs from <i>P. vulgaris</i> (<i>PvGLO</i>
                  <sup><i>P1</i></sup>, <i>PvGLO</i>
                  <sup><i>P2</i></sup>, and <i>PvGLO</i>
                  <sup><i>T</i></sup>) and <i>P. denticulata</i> (<i>PdGLO1</i> and <i>PdGLO2</i>) to our genome assembly, we find unequivocal support for two distinct <i>GLOBOSA</i> genes in <i>Primula</i>. Specifically, we find that Li <i>et al.</i>’s [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Li J, Webster M, Dudas B, Cook H, Manfield I, Davies B et al (2008) The S locus‐linked Primula homeotic mutant sepaloid shows characteristics of a B‐function mutant but does not result from mutation in a B2 function gene. Plant J 56:1–12" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d17215e2389">60</a>] two putative L-morph alleles (named <i>PvGLO</i>
                  <sup><i>P1</i></sup> and <i>PvGLO</i>
                  <sup><i>P2</i></sup> by the authors) and <i>PdGLO1</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 91" title="Viaene T, Vekemans D, Irish VF, Geeraerts A, Huysmans S, Janssens S et al (2009) Pistillata–duplications as a mode for floral diversification in (Basal) asterids. Mol Biol Evol 26:2627–45" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR91" id="ref-link-section-d17215e2410">91</a>] map to an entirely different scaffold than the putative S-morph allele (<i>PvGLO</i>
                  <sup><i>T</i></sup> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Li J, Webster M, Dudas B, Cook H, Manfield I, Davies B et al (2008) The S locus‐linked Primula homeotic mutant sepaloid shows characteristics of a B‐function mutant but does not result from mutation in a B2 function gene. Plant J 56:1–12" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d17215e2421">60</a>]) and <i>PdGLO2</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 91" title="Viaene T, Vekemans D, Irish VF, Geeraerts A, Huysmans S, Janssens S et al (2009) Pistillata–duplications as a mode for floral diversification in (Basal) asterids. Mol Biol Evol 26:2627–45" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR91" id="ref-link-section-d17215e2427">91</a>]. We hereby refer to these two genes as <i>PveGLO1</i> (BG8816827), which is homologous to <i>PdGLO1</i> and the two alleles <i>PvGLO</i>
                  <sup><i>P1</i></sup> and <i>PvGLO</i>
                  <sup><i>P2</i></sup> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Li J, Webster M, Dudas B, Cook H, Manfield I, Davies B et al (2008) The S locus‐linked Primula homeotic mutant sepaloid shows characteristics of a B‐function mutant but does not result from mutation in a B2 function gene. Plant J 56:1–12" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d17215e2451">60</a>], and <i>PveGLO2</i> (BG8816696), which is homologous to <i>PdGLO2</i> and <i>PvGLO</i>
                  <sup><i>T</i></sup> (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#Fig3">3</a>). Viaene <i>et al.</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 91" title="Viaene T, Vekemans D, Irish VF, Geeraerts A, Huysmans S, Janssens S et al (2009) Pistillata–duplications as a mode for floral diversification in (Basal) asterids. Mol Biol Evol 26:2627–45" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR91" id="ref-link-section-d17215e2475">91</a>] failed to find evidence for two <i>GLOBOSA</i> homologs in a monomorphic species of Primulaceae (<i>Cyclamen persicum</i>), and thus it is possible that the duplicated <i>GLOBOSA</i> could be specific to the genus <i>Primula</i>. By comparing the mRNA sequences of <i>PvGLO</i>
                  <sup><i>T</i></sup> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Li J, Webster M, Dudas B, Cook H, Manfield I, Davies B et al (2008) The S locus‐linked Primula homeotic mutant sepaloid shows characteristics of a B‐function mutant but does not result from mutation in a B2 function gene. Plant J 56:1–12" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d17215e2498">60</a>], <i>PdGLO2</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 91" title="Viaene T, Vekemans D, Irish VF, Geeraerts A, Huysmans S, Janssens S et al (2009) Pistillata–duplications as a mode for floral diversification in (Basal) asterids. Mol Biol Evol 26:2627–45" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR91" id="ref-link-section-d17215e2504">91</a>], and <i>PveGLO2</i> assembled from S-morph RNAseq data with the scaffold sequence carrying <i>PveGLO2</i> (Contig1404), we find that the last exon and 3’ UTR appear inverted in the genome assembly. This inversion in the scaffold sequence is likely the product of an assembly error, because the transcriptome assembly contains a complete <i>PveGLO2</i> gene and there is a large (approximately 10 Kb) assembly gap within the last intron of the <i>PveGLO2</i> gene model. <i>PveGLO2</i> is completely silenced in L-morph flowers of <i>P. veris</i> and <i>P. vulgaris</i>, which - in light of Li <i>et al.</i>’s [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Li J, Webster M, Dudas B, Cook H, Manfield I, Davies B et al (2008) The S locus‐linked Primula homeotic mutant sepaloid shows characteristics of a B‐function mutant but does not result from mutation in a B2 function gene. Plant J 56:1–12" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d17215e2533">60</a>] finding of linkage between <i>PveGLO2</i> (<i>PveGLO</i>
                  <sup><i>T</i></sup> in their terminology) and the S-morph haplotype of the <i>S</i>-locus in <i>P. vulgaris</i> - could reflect the absence of this gene from the L-morph haplotype. Thus, while we currently do not have evidence for <i>PveGLO2</i> being linked to the <i>S</i>-locus in <i>P. veris</i> (see below), this duplicated B-function MADS-box gene is an attractive candidate for future studies of distylous flower development in <i>Primula</i>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Figure 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-014-0567-z/MediaObjects/13059_2014_567_Fig3_HTML.gif?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-014-0567-z/MediaObjects/13059_2014_567_Fig3_HTML.gif" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>
                          <b>Sequence alignment and tree showing the relationship of</b>
                          <b><i>GLOBOSA</i></b>
                          <b>/</b>
                          <b><i>PISTILLATA</i></b>
                          <b>genes in Primulaceae. (A)</b> Amino acid positions that are shaded are identical to <i>PveGLO2</i>. <b>(B)</b> The tree was rooted with the <i>PI</i> gene sequence from the kiwifruit draft genome (<i>A. chinensis</i>, [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Huang S, Ding J, Deng D, Tang W, Sun H, Liu D et al (2013) Draft genome of the kiwifruit Actinidia chinensis. Nat Commun 4:1–9" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d17215e2603">69</a>]). Branch support values were calculated based on 1,000 neighbor-joining bootstrap replicates. Our labelling of <i>GLO1</i> and <i>GLO2</i> follows the convention established by Viaene <i>et al.</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 91" title="Viaene T, Vekemans D, Irish VF, Geeraerts A, Huysmans S, Janssens S et al (2009) Pistillata–duplications as a mode for floral diversification in (Basal) asterids. Mol Biol Evol 26:2627–45" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR91" id="ref-link-section-d17215e2616">91</a>]. See text for details regarding gene names.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Bulk Segregant RAD-Seq identifies SNPs linked to the <i>P. veris S-</i>locus</h3><p>To enable testing of the above hypotheses about properties of <i>S-</i>locus linked sequences, we aimed to experimentally identify corresponding scaffolds. To this end, we searched for sequence polymorphisms tightly linked to the <i>S</i>-locus in <i>P. veris</i>, using a RAD-seq bulk segregant analysis on pooled L-morph and S-morph DNA. Any polymorphisms that are closely linked to the <i>S-</i>locus should be largely homozygous in the L-morph pool, but heterozygous in the S-morph pool. Using a stringent set of criteria (coverage in both pools &gt;60×, allele frequency in L-morph pool 1.0, allele frequency in S-morph pool 0.3-0.7), this analysis identified 24 SNPs as potentially tightly linked to the <i>S</i> locus (see Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#MOESM6">6</a>).</p><p>To enable high-throughput genotyping, the SNPs identified above were converted to PCR-based markers, either cleaved-amplified polymorphic sequences (CAPS) or derived CAPS (dCAPS). These markers were initially tested on pooled L- or S-morph DNA. Out of the 24 markers, 13 allowed for robust amplification and detected the predicted polymorphisms between L- and S-morph pools. These markers were next used to genotype 48 individuals from a natural <i>P. veris</i> population in Potsdam, Germany, and based on these data, six markers were found to be linked to the <i>S-</i>locus, while the remaining seven showed no clear correlation between marker genotype and <i>S</i>-locus phenotype (that is, floral morph; Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#Fig4">4</a>). Inspection of the genotypes in Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#Fig4">4</a>A indicates that markers 9274, 37812, 41358, 51955, 59102, and 101982 are completely linked to the <i>S-</i>locus when considering only the L-morph plants. The genotypes of S-morph plants suggest that in the population there are in fact two common chromosomes harboring the dominant <i>S</i>-allele (S-morph). The first is found in phenotypically S-morph plants that are heterozygous for all six markers in question and thus carries S-morph specific alleles at these markers; this chromosome will be termed the ‘original S-morph chromosome’ below. The other common chromosome is found in phenotypically S-morph plants homozygous for the L-morph allele at markers 9274, 37812, 41358, and 101982. Hence, a recombination event appears to have combined the dominant <i>S</i> allele with the recessive <i>s</i> (L-morph) alleles at these four markers, and the resulting ‘recombinant S-morph chromosome’ appears to have spread in the local population. No recombination was found among the four markers 9274, 37812, 41358, and 101982. Thus, a tentative, relative marker order deduced from these 48 samples is: (<i>S-</i>locus/51955/59102) - (37812/101982/9274/41358), where the markers/loci in brackets cannot be separated from each other. From these six markers we chose four (51955, 59102, 37812, 101982) and genotyped 91 additional individuals (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#Fig4">4</a>B). No further recombinant genotypes beyond the ones found in the first 48 plants were detected, thus confirming the deduced order of loci. By blasting the six loci to the <i>P. veris</i> genome assembly, we find that markers 37812 and 101982 are both located on Contig437, a result consistent with their complete linkage in the mapping results. Notably, we also find that markers 9274 and 59102 are separated by approximately 70 Kb on Contig273. Our mapping results suggest that the recombination event that may have given rise to the ‘recombinant S-morph chromosome’ could have occurred between 9274 and 59102 on Contig273, and this region can thus be considered a functional boundary of the <i>S</i>-locus. We also note that the absence of any detectable recombination between four markers (37812, 101982, 9274, and 41358) located on three different scaffolds of considerable size outside of the functional <i>S-</i>locus indicates that the suppression of recombination extends over large distances beyond the <i>S-</i>locus (see Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#Tab2">2</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Figure 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-014-0567-z/MediaObjects/13059_2014_567_Fig4_HTML.gif?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-014-0567-z/MediaObjects/13059_2014_567_Fig4_HTML.gif" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>
                          <b>Identification of</b>
                          <b><i>S</i></b>
                          <b>-locus linked SNPs.</b> Graphical representation of genotypes of <b>(A)</b> 48 <i>P. veris</i> plants genotyped for 13 PCR-based markers derived from the bulk segregant RAD sequencing analysis and three SNPs in <i>SLL1</i>, and <b>(B)</b> 91 <i>P. veris</i> plants genotyped for four PCR-based SNP markers and three SNPs in the <i>SLL1</i> gene. Phenotype: yellow = L-morph, green = S-morph. Genotypes: yellow = homozygous for allele 1, green = heterozygous, blue = homozygous for allele 2.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                  <div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Table 2 
                          <b>Genome scaffolds putatively linked to the</b>
                          <b><i>P. veris S</i></b>
                          <b>-locus</b>
                        </b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z/tables/2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                <p>To compare the resolution afforded by our mapping in the natural <i>P. veris</i> population to previous reports, we isolated the <i>P. veris</i> locus orthologous to the previously described <i>PvSLL1</i> locus from <i>P. vulgaris</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Li J, Webster M, Furuya M, Gilmartin P (2007) Identification and characterization of pin and thrum alleles of two genes that co‐segregate with the Primula S locus. Plant J 51:18–31" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d17215e3398">49</a>]. Comparing the sequences of the <i>P. veris</i> and the <i>P. vulgaris SLL1</i> locus indicated that one of the internal repeats found in <i>P. vulgaris</i> is replaced by an unrelated sequence in <i>P. veris</i> (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#MOESM7">7</a>: Figure S3). Sequencing this locus from pools of L- and S-morph individuals identified three polymorphic SNPs that were converted to PCR-based genotyping markers. Testing the 139 samples above for these three SNPs indicated no association of any of the SNPs with the <i>S-</i>locus genotype, nor an association of any of the resulting seven haplotypes present in the sample with the <i>S-</i>locus genotype (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#Fig4">4</a>). Although there was still significant linkage disequilibrium between the three SNPs (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#MOESM8">8</a>: Figure S4; Χ<sup>2</sup>-test; <i>P</i> &lt;0.0001), we identified a substantial proportion of recombinant genotypes, even though the three SNPs are all found within less than 200 bp. This suggests that the studied population captures a very large number of historical recombination events resulting in an absence of LD between <i>PvSLL1</i> and the <i>S</i> locus. Conversely, it indicates that the very tightly linked markers identified above are likely to reside within or immediately next to the <i>S-</i>locus.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Genome-wide estimates of heterozygosity and polymorphism</h3><p>Heterozygosity and polymorphism were explored in the <i>P. veris</i> genome by resequencing a single <i>P. veris</i> individual from a Latvian population and mapping these raw sequence reads to our genome assembly. We found a total of 1,184,748 SNPs (0.51%) composed of 982,590 heterozygous sites and 202,158 homozygous sites compared to the reference assembly, and a total of 96,113 indels were identified in the Latvian <i>P. veris</i> individual (see Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#MOESM9">9</a>).</p><p>Theory predicts that the <i>S-</i>locus will be largely shielded from recombination [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 92" title="Uyenoyama M (2005) Evolution under tight linkage to mating type. New Phytol 165:63–70" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR92" id="ref-link-section-d17215e3471">92</a>], and thus <i>S</i>-linked genome scaffolds are expected to show elevated rates of heterozygosity in S-morph plants, which are heterozygous at the <i>S</i>-locus (Ss), compared to L-morph plants, which are homozygous at the <i>S</i>-locus (ss). We evaluated this prediction by mapping RNAseq reads from L- and S-morph plants of <i>P. veris</i> to our genome assembly to compare the number of SNPs predicted in coding sequences of genome scaffolds that are putatively linked to the <i>S</i>-locus. We found a total of 45,018 SNPs when mapping the L-morph RNA to the genome assembly, and 51,191 SNPs when mapping the S-morph RNA to the genome assembly, and the average S-morph/L-morph SNP ratio for all genome scaffolds is 2.54 (variance = 23.78). Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#Tab2">2</a> shows the results of this analysis for specific genome scaffolds carrying genes that have been previously identified as linked to the <i>S</i>-locus in <i>P. vulgaris</i>: <i>PvSLL1</i>, <i>PvSLL2</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Li J, Webster M, Furuya M, Gilmartin P (2007) Identification and characterization of pin and thrum alleles of two genes that co‐segregate with the Primula S locus. Plant J 51:18–31" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d17215e3506">49</a>], <i>PveGLO1</i>, and <i>PveGLO2</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Li J, Webster M, Dudas B, Cook H, Manfield I, Davies B et al (2008) The S locus‐linked Primula homeotic mutant sepaloid shows characteristics of a B‐function mutant but does not result from mutation in a B2 function gene. Plant J 56:1–12" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d17215e3515">60</a>]. Additionally, we include the genome scaffolds carrying the six loci that are linked to the <i>S</i>-locus based on our RAD-seq bulk segregant analysis and fine mapping experiments (see above): RAD loci 41,358, 37,812/101,982, 51,955, and 9,274/59,102. Our results show that, on average, there are more SNPs mapped from S-morph RNA reads than L-morph RNA reads on putatively <i>S</i>-linked scaffolds (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#Tab2">2</a>). By utilizing RNAseq reads rather than whole genome sequence data, our analysis is limited to polymorphisms evident in coding regions alone. Additionally, if certain genes experience high degrees of allele-specific expression, we will fail to observe polymorphisms solely due to an absence of mapped reads. It is important to note here that Contig1404, which carries just one gene (<i>PveGLO2</i>), contains no polymorphism when mapping either L- or S-morph RNAseq reads: this is the expected result if <i>PveGLO2</i> were <i>S</i>-linked and only expressed in the <i>S</i>-locus of the S-morph. The expectation of increased heterozygosity is fundamentally based on the assumption that individuals with an L-morph phenotype will also be highly homozygous at the <i>S</i>-locus. But if recombination is suppressed between L-morph alleles as well, then one might actually expect that linked polymorphisms would accumulate between different L-morph haplotypes, thus leading to elevated heterozygosity at the <i>S</i>-locus in L-morph as well as S-morph plants. Addressing this question is beyond the scope of the current study, but future analyses aimed at more precisely characterizing genomic patterns of heterozygosity in and around the <i>S</i>-locus will benefit greatly from our draft genome assembly of <i>P. veris</i>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Conclusions"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Conclusions</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><p>Our <i>P. veris</i> genome assembly exemplifies the power of high-throughput DNA sequencing technologies and establishes a benchmark for the rapid <i>de novo</i> assembly of a highly heterozygous, non-model plant with a moderately sized genome (that is, 479.22 Mb). We believe that the primary strength of our sequencing strategy lies in the diversity of sequence libraries and sequencing platforms we have employed. Our study suggests great promise in the application of PacBio long-read data for the improvement of <i>de novo</i> genome assemblies, and it is possible that, with the direct incorporation of the raw PacBio sequences in the assembly process, even more information could be extracted from long reads. Currently such integration is strongly limited by the <i>a priori</i> correction of raw PacBio reads, which significantly reduces the quantity of usable data resulting from a PacBio run (G. Russo, personal observation). In the case of large eukaryotic genomes, this translates into the need for a largely unfeasible sequencing throughput.</p><p>Using our <i>de novo</i> genome assembly coupled with RNAseq data from flower buds, we have identified 113 genes that show significant morph-specific differential expression in both <i>P. veris</i> and <i>P. vulgaris</i>. Functional analysis of the list of candidate genes has revealed clusters of GO terms related to extracellular processes, cell growth and organization, and development of reproductive structures. Furthermore, our genome assembly shows that the B-function MADS box gene <i>GLOBOSA</i> has been duplicated in <i>Primula</i>, and we find that one of these copies (<i>PveGLO2</i>) is silenced in L-morph flower buds, but we still do not know if <i>PveGLO2</i> is linked to the <i>S</i>-locus, and thus a suitable candidate gene for morph-specific floral development. Future work toward characterizing this candidate gene could involve resequencing both L- and S-morph plants to evaluate the presence of this gene in both morphs and identify morph-specific SNPs that can be tested for <i>S</i>-locus linkage in a mapping population.</p><p>Employing a bulk segregant analysis followed by high-resolution mapping, we identify six loci on four genome scaffolds that are tightly linked to the <i>S</i>-locus in <i>P. veris</i>. When examining these <i>S</i>-linked genome scaffolds as well as genome scaffolds carrying genes previously identified as linked to the <i>S</i>-locus, we find elevated heterozygosity in S-morph versus L-morph coding sequences, consistent with theoretical predictions. Future work toward defining the recombinational limits and genetic composition of the <i>S</i>-locus would benefit greatly from the construction of a linkage map using genetic markers anchored within genome scaffolds. Beyond characterizing the <i>Primula S</i>-locus, our <i>de novo</i> genome sequence will prove to be a valuable resource for marker design and the analysis of population genomic and phylogenomic data. The genomic resources presented here thus represent a significant leap forward in the development of <i>P. veris</i> and <i>P. vulgaris</i> as models in the study of distyly, climatic adaptation, and speciation genetics.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Materials and methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Materials and methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">DNA isolation, library preparation, and sequencing</h3><p>Genomic DNA was extracted from one L-morph and one S-morph individual of <i>P. veris</i> individuals from a cultivated line maintained by the Vogt Stauden nursery [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 93" title="Vogt Stauden. &#xA;                    http://www.vogtstauden.ch/&#xA;                    &#xA;                  . Accessed 21 Dec 2014." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR93" id="ref-link-section-d17215e3651">93</a>]. Genomic DNA was extracted from young leaf tissue using two different protocols. The CelLytic PN Isolation/Extraction Kit (Sigma-Aldrich, Buchs, Switzerland) was used to partially enrich nuclear DNA by following the manufacturer’s protocol for ‘Crude Preparation of Nuclei’, and the resulting product was further purified using a standard CTAB DNA extraction protocol [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 94" title="Murray MG, Thompson WF (1980) Rapid isolation of high molecular weight plant DNA. Nucleic Acids Res 8:4321–5" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR94" id="ref-link-section-d17215e3654">94</a>]. This genomic DNA is herein referred to as ‘partially enriched genomic DNA’. A second sample of ‘total genomic DNA’ was directly extracted from young leaves following the standard CTAB DNA extraction protocol [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 94" title="Murray MG, Thompson WF (1980) Rapid isolation of high molecular weight plant DNA. Nucleic Acids Res 8:4321–5" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR94" id="ref-link-section-d17215e3657">94</a>]. For use in estimating the genomic levels of polymorphism (see below), total genomic DNA was extracted from a <i>P. veris</i> individual from a population in the Mazsalaca Municipality in Latvia (57.91494°N, 24.98437°E). The floral morph of this plant was not noted when the sample was collected, and thus we do not know if this was a L- or S-morph individual. All DNA extractions were quantified using the Broad Range DNA Kit on a Qubit v.2.0 spectrophotometer (Life Technologies, Zug, Switzerland), and the presence of high molecular weight DNA was confirmed by 1% agarose-gel electrophoresis stained with 1% ethidium bromide.</p><p>Genomic DNA was prepared for sequencing by generating three different types of libraries. An overview of the library types employed for the assembly and their summary statistics are shown in Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#Tab1">1</a>. The small fragment library was prepared using the Illumina Paired-End DNA protocol. The average fragment size was 180 nucleotides and the fragments were sequenced on an Illumina HiSeq 2000 in paired-end mode. The length of 100 bases for both the forward and reverse reads ensured a small overlap across the read pairs, a feature recommended for <i>de novo</i> assembly. Mate-pair libraries were produced by circularizing DNA fragments of different lengths using a biotinylated internal adapter. The circular fragments were then sheared and the biotinylated segments were detected and amplified for sequencing. Illumina Nextera long mate-pair protocol and Life Technologies mate-pair protocol, which are based on the above-mentioned approach, were both applied and the resulting libraries were sequenced on Illumina HiSeq2000 and MiSeq instruments and on Life Technologies Ion Proton System, respectively. Libraries prepared from fragments of three different sizes (3 Kb, 6 Kb, and 9 Kb) were selected for sequencing on the Illumina technologies, whereas only libraries generated from 3 Kb fragments were sequenced on the Ion Proton. Finally, long fragment DNA templates were produced following the Pacific Biosciences (PacBio) 10 kb library protocol and sequenced on 25 SMRT cells of the PacBio RS II.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec13">Genome assembly</h3><p>Fragments sequenced in paired-end mode on the Illumina HiSeq were stripped of the sequencing adapters without further preprocessing, due to the high quality of the sequencing run. Sequences produced from the mate-pair libraries had both external and internal adapters removed. Mate-pairs resulting from templates sequenced on platforms generating reads of variable length (that is, MiSeq and IonProton) were retained only if both the forward and the reverse reads contained at least 30 nucleotides after adapter removal. Long fragments sequenced on the Pacific Bioscience RSII had the SMRTbell adapters removed and filtered according to a minimum read length of 50 nucleotides and a minimum read quality of 75%.</p><p>The final draft of the <i>P.veris</i> genome was obtained using a two-step procedure.</p><p>
                  <i>De novo</i> assembly was performed using ALLPATHS-LG v48579 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 95" title="Gnerre S, MacCallum I, Przybylski D, Ribeiro FJ, Burton JN, Walker BJ et al (2011) High-quality draft assemblies of mammalian genomes from massively parallel sequence data. Proc Natl Acad Sci 108:1513–8" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR95" id="ref-link-section-d17215e3692">95</a>], with specific settings for a diploid organism (that is, by setting PLOIDY = 2) and provided with a genome size estimate of 445 Mb [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Siljak-Yakovlev S, Pustahija F, Bogunić F, Šolić EM, Muratović E, Bašić N et al (2010) Towards a genome size and chromosome number database of Balkan flora: C-values in 343 taxa with novel values for 242. Adv Sci Lett 3:190–213" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d17215e3695">65</a>]. Data from the genomic templates sequenced on the Pacific Biosciences (PacBio) RS II were not used at this stage, as ALLPATHS-LG relies on paired reads information (either paired-end or mate pairs) and discourages the use of long fragment reads when assembling large eukaryotic organisms. Long fragment reads were only successively incorporated to perform gap-closing and further scaffolding of the first assembly draft using PBJelly v14.1.15 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="English AC, Richards S, Han Y, Wang M, Vee V, Qu J et al (2012) Mind the gap: upgrading genomes with Pacific biosciences RS long-read sequencing technology. PLoS One 7:e47768" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d17215e3698">67</a>]. The PacBio read-alignment software ‘blasr’ was provided with the following settings: <i>minMatch</i> = 8, <i>sdpTupleSize</i> = 8, <i>minPctIdentity</i> =  75, <i>bestn</i> = 1, <i>nCandidates</i> = 10, <i>maxScore</i> = 500, and <i>noSplitSubreads</i> was activated.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec14">RNA isolation, library preparation, and sequencing</h3><p>With the aim of sequencing a diverse transcript pool, total RNA was isolated separately from floral and leaf tissues for each plant sampled in our study. Entire, mature floral buds, 3 to 5 days prior to anthesis were used for RNA extraction from floral tissues. Leaf tissues were sampled approximately 4 to 6 days prior to complete leaf blade expansion. RNA was isolated from a <i>P. veris</i> L-morph plant (Vogt 026-P) and from a <i>P. veris</i> S-morph plant (VE8-T). For comparative analyses, we also extracted floral bud RNA from <i>P. vulgaris</i> L-morph (VU2-P) and S-morph (VU-T37) plants 3 to 5 days prior to anthesis. Total RNA was extracted using the RNAqueous Kit (Life Technologies, Zug, Switzerland) following the manufacturer’s standard protocol. Extracted RNA was quantified using a Qubit v2 fluorometer RNA assay kit (Life Technologies) and diluted to 40 ng/μL for subsequent library construction. For all six samples, the standard unstranded Illumina TruSeq protocol was used, which includes a step to pull down polyadenylated transcripts from the cDNA pool. Libraries were then multiplexed using a set of six Illumina barcodes and sequenced on one lane of an Illumina HiSeq 2000 instrument in paired-end mode.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec15">Transcriptome assembly and comparative analysis</h3><p>Transcriptome assemblies were generated for each species using either the raw Illumina sequence data from <i>P. veris</i> and <i>P. vulgaris</i> RNAseq runs or data downloaded from Genbank for <i>P. obconica</i>, <i>P. wilsonii</i>, and <i>P. poissonii</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Zhang L, Yan H-F, Wu W, Yu H, Ge X-J (2013) Comparative transcriptome analysis and marker development of two closely related Primrose species (Primula poissonii and Primula wilsonii). BMC Genomics 14:329" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d17215e3764">66</a>]. Adaptor sequences were removed from the raw sequencing reads, and reads with a quality score below 15 were removed. <i>De novo</i> transcriptome assembly was performed using the Trinity software suite v.2013-8-14 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 96" title="Grabherr MG, Haas BJ, Yassour M, Levin JZ, Thompson DA, Amit I et al (2011) Full-length transcriptome assembly from RNA-Seq data without a reference genome. Nat Biotechnol 29:644–52" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR96" id="ref-link-section-d17215e3770">96</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 97" title="Haas BJ, Papanicolaou A, Yassour M, Grabherr M, Blood PD, Bowden J et al (2013) De novo transcript sequence reconstruction from RNA-seq using the Trinity platform for reference generation and analysis. Nat Protoc 8:1494–512" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR97" id="ref-link-section-d17215e3773">97</a>] using default parameters. The likely coding DNA sequences (CDS) and corresponding proteins within the <i>de novo</i> transcriptome assembly were estimated with Transdecoder [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 98" title="TransDecoder. &#xA;                    http://transdecoder.sourceforge.net/&#xA;                    &#xA;                  . Accessed 21 Dec 2014." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR98" id="ref-link-section-d17215e3779">98</a>]. Redundant transcripts potentially representing sequencing errors or genetic polymorphisms were clustered with the cd-hit software [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 99" title="Fu L, Niu B, Zhu Z, Wu S, Li W (2012) CD-HIT: accelerated for clustering the next-generation sequencing data. Bioinformatics 28:3150–2" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR99" id="ref-link-section-d17215e3783">99</a>].</p><p>Protein sequences from the cd-hit results of the <i>P. veris</i>, <i>P. vulgaris</i>, <i>P. obconica</i>, <i>P. wilsonii</i>, and <i>P. poissonii</i> transcriptomes were used to identify clusters of putatively orthologous genes using OrthoMCL v.2.0.9 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Li L (2003) OrthoMCL: Identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res 13:2178–89" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d17215e3805">79</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 80" title="Fischer S, Brunk BP, Chen F, Gao X, Harb OS, Iodice JB et al (2011) Using OrthoMCL to assign proteins to OrthoMCL-DB groups or to cluster proteomes into new ortholog groups. John Wiley &amp; Sons, Inc., Hoboken, NJ" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR80" id="ref-link-section-d17215e3808">80</a>]. Pairwise sequence similarities between all protein sequences were calculated with blastp using an e-value cutoff of 1e-05. Using these results, protein clusters that are roughly equivalent to orthologous groups were estimated using the Markov clustering algorithm employed in OrthoMCL with the default inflation value (−I) of 1.5. A second OrthoMCL analysis was performed using identical parameters in order to identify putative orthologs with <i>P. veris</i> among a broader sample of sequenced and annotated Euasteridae genomes, including tomato (<i>Solanum lycopersicum</i>; [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Tomato Genome Consortium (2012) The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature 485:635–41" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d17215e3817">68</a>]), grape (<i>Vitis vinifera</i>; [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Jaillon O, Aury J-M, Noel B, Policriti A, Clepet C, Casagrande A et al (2007) The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449:463–7" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d17215e3824">70</a>]), kiwifruit (<i>Actinidia chinensis</i>; [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Huang S, Ding J, Deng D, Tang W, Sun H, Liu D et al (2013) Draft genome of the kiwifruit Actinidia chinensis. Nat Commun 4:1–9" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d17215e3830">69</a>]), and monkeyflower (<i>Mimulus guttatus</i>; Mimulus Genome Project, DoE Joint Genome Institute).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec16">Gene prediction and annotation</h3><p>Genome annotation was performed using the Maker2 pipeline [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Holt C, Yandell M (2011) MAKER2: an annotation pipeline and genome-database management tool for second-generation genome projects. BMC Bioinformatics 12:491" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d17215e3845">78</a>]. Augustus v2.7 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 100" title="Stanke M, Diekhans M, Baertsch R, Haussler D (2008) Using native and syntenically mapped cDNA alignments to improve de novo gene finding. Bioinformatics 24:637–44" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR100" id="ref-link-section-d17215e3848">100</a>] and SNAP (Release 2013-11-29; [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 101" title="Korf I (2004) Gene finding in novel genomes. BMC Bioinformatics 5:59" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR101" id="ref-link-section-d17215e3851">101</a>]) were used as <i>ab initio</i> gene predictors. The Trinity-assembled <i>P. veris</i> transcripts (see above) were used as transcript evidence. Protein sequences from the <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> genome (version TAIR10) were used as homology-based evidence. Repetitive genomic elements were identified and masked from annotation with RepeatMasker [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 102" title="RepeatMasker. &#xA;                    http://www.repeatmasker.org&#xA;                    &#xA;                  . Accessed 21 Dec 2014." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR102" id="ref-link-section-d17215e3864">102</a>] using the Repbase database [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 103" title="Jurka J, Kapitonov VV, Pavlicek A, Klonowski P, Kohany O, Walichiewicz J (2005) Repbase Update, a database of eukaryotic repetitive elements. Cytogenet Genome Res 110:462–7" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR103" id="ref-link-section-d17215e3867">103</a>]. The Maker2 annotation was first run using the <i>de novo</i> transcriptome directly to infer gene predictions (that is, est2genome = 1), and training files for the <i>ab initio</i> gene predictors Augustus and SNAP were generated based on these results. The annotation pipeline was then run iteratively two additional times using the assembled <i>P. veris</i> transcriptome as evidence (that is, est2genome = 0) and providing new training files with each run. At this point, the protein-homology set was broadened to include all Uniprot proteins in the Embryophyta and a final annotation was performed by also providing Maker2 with the transcript-annotation file (in .gff format) obtained from the previous runs (that is, ‘pass-through’ aided annotation). InterProScan v.5 was used to identify PFAM domains [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 104" title="Punta M, Coggill PC, Eberhardt RY, Mistry J, Tate J, Boursnell C et al (2011) The Pfam protein families database. Nucleic Acids Res 40:D290–301" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR104" id="ref-link-section-d17215e3880">104</a>] in the predicted <i>P. veris</i> protein sequences using default parameters [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 105" title="Quevillon E, Silventoinen V, Pillai S, Harte N, Mulder N, Apweiler R et al (2005) InterProScan: protein domains identifier. Nucleic Acids Res 33:W116–20" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR105" id="ref-link-section-d17215e3886">105</a>]. The predicted <i>P. veris</i> protein sequences were searched against a set of 248 highly conserved core eukaryotic genes using the CEGMA pipeline [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Parra G, Bradnam K, Korf I (2007) CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes. Bioinformatics 23:1061–7" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d17215e3892">77</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec17">Estimating heterozygosity and polymorphism</h3><p>To estimate genome-wide patterns of heterozygosity and polymorphism, a single <i>P. veris</i> individual from near the northern range limit of the species in Latvia (see above) was resequenced. A total of 158.8 million paired-end reads were generated on one-third of a single lane of an Illumina HiSeq instrument, yielding about 16 Gb of data. The resulting reads were mapped to the newly constructed <i>P. veris</i> reference assembly using Bowtie2 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 106" title="Langmead B, Salzberg SL (2012) Fast gapped-read alignment with Bowtie 2. Nat Meth 9:357–9" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR106" id="ref-link-section-d17215e3909">106</a>] with paired-end settings and <i>--local-sensitive</i> parameters. Insertion/deletions and SNPs were called using a combination of SAMtools v0.1.19 and Bcftools v0.1.19 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 107" title="Li H, Handsaker B, Wysoker A, Fennell T, Ruan J, Homer N et al (2009) The Sequence Alignment/Map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics 25:2078–9" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR107" id="ref-link-section-d17215e3915">107</a>] with default settings. Polymorphisms reported as purely diploid with a minimum coverage of 30×, a maximum coverage of 300×, and a minimum quality score of 50 were retained.</p><p>Genomic regions within and tightly linked to the S-locus are expected to be more heterozygous in S-morph than in L-morph plants [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 92" title="Uyenoyama M (2005) Evolution under tight linkage to mating type. New Phytol 165:63–70" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR92" id="ref-link-section-d17215e3921">92</a>]. To identify genome scaffolds with elevated heterozygosity in coding regions of S-morph versus L-morph plants, we mapped the raw RNAseq reads from L-morph and S-morph individuals back to the <i>P. veris</i> genome assembly. The positions of SNPs and indels in the <i>P. veris</i> draft genome assembly are provided in Additional files <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#MOESM9">9</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#MOESM10">10</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec18">Gene expression differences between floral morphs</h3><p>In order to perform differential expression analysis, the final assembly was used as reference genome and the genomic features annotated by Maker2 acted as gtf annotation file.</p><p>The reads produced by sequencing the six RNA samples were mapped using STAR aligner [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 108" title="Dobin A, Davis CA, Schlesinger F, Drenkow J, Zaleski C, Jha S et al (2012) STAR: ultrafast universal RNA-seq aligner. Bioinformatics 29:15–21" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR108" id="ref-link-section-d17215e3947">108</a>] with default parameter settings. CountOverlap from the Bioconductor package GenomicRanges [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 109" title="Lawrence M, Huber W, Pagès H, Aboyoun P, Carlson M, Gentleman R et al (2013) Software for computing and annotating genomic ranges. PLoS Comput Biol 9:e1003118" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR109" id="ref-link-section-d17215e3950">109</a>] was used to summarize the overlaps between the reads and the annotated features, and based on this information the Bioconductor tool edgeR [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 110" title="Robinson MD, Smyth GK. edgeR: a Bioconductor package for differential expression analysis of digital gene expression data. Bioinformatics. 2009;26:139–40." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR110" id="ref-link-section-d17215e3953">110</a>] was employed to test for differentially expressed genes. Three pairwise comparisons were performed: (1) L- and S-morph floral RNA from <i>P. veris</i>; (2) L- and S-morph floral RNA from <i>P. vulgaris</i>; and (3) L- and S-morph leaf RNA from <i>P. veris</i>. Genes were determined to exhibit significant differential expression between samples if the <i>P</i> value (corrected for FDR) was below 0.05. Further validation of differential expression was accomplished by cross-referencing the <i>P. veris</i> L-morph versus S-morph floral genes with <i>P. vulgaris</i> L-morph versus S-morph floral genes. The set of cross-referenced genes showing significant morph-specific differential expression was examined for enrichment of gene ontology (GO) terms using the DAVID Bioinformatics Resources v6.7 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 81" title="Huang DW, Sherman BT, Lempicki RA (2008) Systematic and integrative analysis of large gene lists using DAVID bioinformatics resources. Nat Protoc 4:44–57" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR81" id="ref-link-section-d17215e3975">81</a>]. The set of 113 <i>P. veris</i> genes showing morph-specific differential expression was searched against <i>A. thaliana</i> proteins (TAIR10) to identify homologous genes using blastp with an e-value cutoff of 10e-3. A total of 92 <i>A. thaliana</i> homologues were confidently identified, and the remaining 21 genes were not included in the GO terms analysis. Functional annotation clustering and enrichment analyses were performed with DAVID using default settings and the default <i>A. thaliana</i> background.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec19">Bulk segregant analysis to identify loci linked to the <i>P. veris S</i>-locus and PCR-based genotyping</h3><p>Genomic DNA was extracted from two pools of over 100 L-morph or over 100 S-morph individuals collected from a large population of <i>P. veris</i> plants in Park Sanssouci in Potsdam, Germany, following the protocol above (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#Sec12">DNA Isolation, Library Preparation, and Sequencing</a>). Restriction-associated DNA sequencing (RAD-seq; [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 111" title="Baird N, Etter P, Atwood T, Currey M, Shiver A, Lewis Z et al (2008) Rapid SNP discovery and genetic mapping using sequenced RAD markers. PLoS One 3:e3376" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR111" id="ref-link-section-d17215e4009">111</a>]) using <i>Pst</i>I was performed on these pooled genomic DNAs by Floragenex (Oregon, USA). A total of 43 and 52 million Illumina paired-end sequence reads were obtained for the L- and S-morph pools, respectively. From these paired-end sequences, contigs flanking the <i>Pst</i>I-sites were assembled, and reads from the pooled L- and S-morph samples were aligned to these contigs to detect and catalogue polymorphisms. Polymorphisms putatively linked to the <i>S</i>-locus were identified using custom perl scripts that parsed a variant call format (VCF) file cataloging the SNPs observed in the sequenced <i>P. veris</i> pools. Briefly, in order to consider a variant as linked, several criteria needed to be met. First, the sequencing coverage for any candidate allele in both pools needed to exceed 60×. The allele frequency for each variant in both the L-morph and S-morph pools was then estimated by counting the number of sequence reads in each pool harboring either the reference or alternate allele, divided by the total number of reads covering the specific variant in that pool. Note the sequencing coverage and allele counts for all loci are documented in the VCF file. Finally, in order to identify variants strongly linked to the <i>S</i>-locus we screened for variants that had an allele frequency in the L-morph pool of 1.0, while the allele frequency in S-morph pool could range between 0.3 and 0.7.</p><p>To test linkage of candidate polymorphisms to the <i>S</i>-locus, polymorphisms identified from the bulk segregant RAD-seq analysis were converted to CAPS and dCAPS markers using dCAPS Finder 2.0 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 112" title="Neff MM, Turk E, Kalishman M (2002) Web-based primer design for single nucleotide polymorphism analysis. Trends Genet 18:613–5" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR112" id="ref-link-section-d17215e4034">112</a>]. Genomic DNA from individual L- and S-morph plants from the Sanssouci population was isolated using the CTAB method [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 94" title="Murray MG, Thompson WF (1980) Rapid isolation of high molecular weight plant DNA. Nucleic Acids Res 8:4321–5" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#ref-CR94" id="ref-link-section-d17215e4037">94</a>]. PCR products were digested with the appropriate restriction enzymes overnight, before resolving on 3% agarose gels. Details of the primers and corresponding restriction enzymes used are given in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-014-0567-z#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S6.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec20">Data availability</h3><p>The genome assembly and raw sequence data generated in this study are available at NCBI under the BioProject ID PRJNA238546. The data from the bulk segregant RAD-seq analysis are available under NCBI BioProject ID PRJNA268094. The Maker2 annotation files are available for download at Dryad (<a href="http://www.datadryad.org/">http://www.datadryad.org/</a>) under doi:10.5061/dryad.2s200.</p></div></div></section>
